<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Pasteur" redirects here. <p><b>Louis Pasteur</b> <span class="noexcerpt nowraplinks" style="font-size:small; font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Foreign_Member_of_the_Royal_Society" title="Foreign Member of the Royal Society">ForMemRS</a></span> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/uː/: 'oo' in 'goose'">uː</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/ɜːr/: 'ur' in 'fur'">ɜːr</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <small>French: </small><span class="IPA" lang="fr-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[lwi pastœʁ]</a></span>; 27 December 1822 – 28 September 1895) was a French <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microbiologist" title="Microbiologist">microbiologist</a> renowned for his discoveries of the principles of <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">microbial fermentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">pasteurization</a>, the latter of which was named after him. His research in chemistry led to remarkable breakthroughs in the understanding of the causes and preventions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diseases" title="Diseases">diseases</a>, which laid down the foundations of hygiene, public health and much of modern medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup> His works are credited to saving millions of lives through the developments of vaccines for <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a>. He is regarded as one of the founders of modern <a href="/wiki/Bacteriology" title="Bacteriology">bacteriology</a> and has been honored as the "father of bacteriology"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and the "father of <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fein_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-fein-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> (together with <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:9-12">[12]</a></sup> the latter epithet also attributed to <a href="/wiki/Antonie_van_Leeuwenhoek" title="Antonie van Leeuwenhoek">Antonie van Leeuwenhoek</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pasteur was responsible for disproving the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a>. Under the auspices of the <a href="/wiki/French_Academy_of_Sciences" title="French Academy of Sciences">French Academy of Sciences</a>, his experiment demonstrated that in sterilized and sealed flasks, nothing ever developed; conversely, in sterilized but open flasks, microorganisms could grow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seckbach_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-seckbach-14">[14]</a></sup> For this experiment, the academy awarded him the Alhumbert Prize carrying 2,500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francs" title="Francs">francs</a> in 1862.
</p><p>Pasteur is also regarded as one of the fathers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germ_theory_of_diseases" title="Germ theory of diseases">germ theory of diseases</a>, which was a minor medical concept at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ullmann_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-15">[15]</a></sup> His many experiments showed that diseases could be prevented by killing or stopping germs, thereby directly supporting the germ theory and its application in clinical medicine. He is best known to the general public for his invention of the technique of treating <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wine" title="Wine">wine</a> to stop bacterial contamination, a process now called <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">pasteurization</a>. Pasteur also made significant <a href="#Molecular_asymmetry">discoveries</a> in chemistry, most notably on the molecular basis for the <a href="/wiki/Asymmetry" title="Asymmetry">asymmetry</a> of certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystals" title="Crystals">crystals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Racemization" title="Racemization">racemization</a>. Early in his career, his investigation of <a href="/wiki/Tartaric_acid" title="Tartaric acid">tartaric acid</a> resulted in the first resolution of what is now called <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">optical isomers</a>. His work led the way to the current understanding of a fundamental principle in the structure of organic compounds.
</p><p>He was the director of the <a href="/wiki/Pasteur_Institute" title="Pasteur Institute">Pasteur Institute</a>, established in 1887, until his death, and his body was interred in a vault beneath the institute. Although Pasteur made groundbreaking experiments, his reputation became associated with various controversies. Historical reassessment of his notebook revealed that he practiced deception to overcome his rivals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geison1995_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geison1995-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_early_life">Education and early life</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" 2x" width="130"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:141px;max-width:141px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:182px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fran%C3%A7oise_Foliot_-_Mus%C3%A9e_Pasteur_-_Portrait_de_Jeanne-%C3%89tiennette_Roqui.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4676" data-file-width="3565" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Fran%C3%A7oise_Foliot_-_Mus%C3%A9e_Pasteur_-_Portrait_de_Jeanne-%C3%89tiennette_Roqui.jpg/139px-Fran%C3%A7oise_Foliot_-_Mus%C3%A9e_Pasteur_-_Portrait_de_Jeanne-%C3%89tiennette_Roqui.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Fran%C3%A7oise_Foliot_-_Mus%C3%A9e_Pasteur_-_Portrait_de_Jeanne-%C3%89tiennette_Roqui.jpg/209px-Fran%C3%A7oise_Foliot_-_Mus%C3%A9e_Pasteur_-_Portrait_de_Jeanne-%C3%89tiennette_Roqui.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Fran%C3%A7oise_Foliot_-_Mus%C3%A9e_Pasteur_-_Portrait_de_Jeanne-%C3%89tiennette_Roqui.jpg/278px-Fran%C3%A7oise_Foliot_-_Mus%C3%A9e_Pasteur_-_Portrait_de_Jeanne-%C3%89tiennette_Roqui.jpg 2x" width="139"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Portraits of father and mother by Louis Pasteur</div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_Geburtshaus_in_Dole.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Louis_Pasteur_Geburtshaus_in_Dole.jpg/170px-Louis_Pasteur_Geburtshaus_in_Dole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Louis_Pasteur_Geburtshaus_in_Dole.jpg/255px-Louis_Pasteur_Geburtshaus_in_Dole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Louis_Pasteur_Geburtshaus_in_Dole.jpg/340px-Louis_Pasteur_Geburtshaus_in_Dole.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_Geburtshaus_in_Dole.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The house in which Pasteur was born, <a href="/wiki/Dole,_Jura" title="Dole, Jura">Dole</a></div></div></div>
<p>Louis Pasteur was born on 27 December 1822, in <a href="/wiki/Dole,_Jura" title="Dole, Jura">Dole, Jura</a>, France, to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholic" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> family of a poor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tanner_(occupation)" title="Tanner (occupation)">tanner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-catholic_intro_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-catholic_intro-18">[18]</a></sup> He was the third child of Jean-Joseph Pasteur and Jeanne-Etiennette Roqui. The family moved to <a href="/wiki/Marnoz" title="Marnoz">Marnoz</a> in 1826 and then to <a href="/wiki/Arbois" title="Arbois">Arbois</a> in 1827.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Pasteur entered primary school in 1831.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was an average student in his early years, and not particularly academic, as his interests were <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sketch_(drawing)" title="Sketch (drawing)">sketching</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-catholic_intro_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-catholic_intro-18">[18]</a></sup> He drew many pastels and portraits of his parents, friends and neighbors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Pasteur attended secondary school at the Collège d'Arbois.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In October 1838, he left for Paris to join the Pension Barbet, but became homesick and returned in November.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1839, he entered the <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coll%C3%A8ge_Victor-Hugo" title="fr:Collège Victor-Hugo">Collège Royal</a> at <a href="/wiki/Besan%C3%A7on" title="Besançon">Besançon</a> to study philosophy and earned his Bachelor of Letters degree in 1840.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> He was appointed a tutor at the Besançon college while continuing a degree science course with special mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> He failed his first examination in 1841. He managed to pass the <i><a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baccalaur%C3%A9at_scientifique" title="fr:Baccalauréat scientifique">baccalauréat scientifique</a></i> (general science) degree from Dijon, where he earned his Bachelor of Science in Mathematics degree (Bachelier ès Sciences Mathématiques) in 1842,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Math_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Math-1">[1]</a></sup> but with a mediocre grade in chemistry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later in 1842, Pasteur took the entrance test for the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_normale_sup%C3%A9rieure_(Paris)" title="École normale supérieure (Paris)">École Normale Supérieure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He passed the first set of tests, but because his ranking was low, Pasteur decided not to continue and try again next year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> He went back to the Pension Barbet to prepare for the test. He also attended classes at the <a href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e_Saint-Louis" title="Lycée Saint-Louis">Lycée Saint-Louis</a> and lectures of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Dumas" title="Jean-Baptiste Dumas">Jean-Baptiste Dumas</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Sorbonne" title="Sorbonne">Sorbonne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1843, he passed the test with a high ranking and entered the <a href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_normale_sup%C3%A9rieure_(Paris)" title="École normale supérieure (Paris)">École Normale Supérieure</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1845 he received the <i><a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Licence_de_biologie" title="fr:Licence de biologie">licencié ès sciences</a></i> degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debré_and_Forster_p._502_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Debré_and_Forster_p._502-32">[32]</a></sup> In 1846, he was appointed professor of physics at the Collège de Tournon (now called <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e_Gabriel-Faure" title="fr:Lycée Gabriel-Faure">Lycée Gabriel-Faure</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Ard%C3%A8che" title="Ardèche">Ardèche</a>. But the chemist <a href="/wiki/Antoine_J%C3%A9r%C3%B4me_Balard" title="Antoine Jérôme Balard">Antoine Jérôme Balard</a> wanted him back at the <i>École Normale Supérieure</i> as a graduate laboratory assistant (<i>agrégé préparateur</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> He joined Balard and simultaneously started his research in <a href="/wiki/Crystallography" title="Crystallography">crystallography</a> and in 1847, he submitted his two theses, one in chemistry and the other in physics: (a) Chemistry Thesis: "Recherches sur la capacité de saturation de l'acide arsénieux. Etudes des arsénites de potasse, de soude et d'ammoniaque."; (b) Physics Thesis: "1. Études des phénomènes relatifs à la polarisation rotatoire des liquides. 2. Application de la polarisation rotatoire des liquides à la solution de diverses questions de chimie."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thesis_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thesis-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debré_and_Forster_p._502_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Debré_and_Forster_p._502-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>After serving briefly as professor of physics at the Dijon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyc%C3%A9e" title="Lycée">Lycée</a> in 1848, he became professor of chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Strasbourg" title="University of Strasbourg">University of Strasbourg</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> where he met and courted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marie_Laurent" title="Marie Laurent">Marie Laurent</a>, daughter of the university's <a href="/wiki/Rector_(academia)" title="Rector (academia)">rector</a> in 1849. They were married on 29 May 1849,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> and together had five children, only two of whom survived to adulthood;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> the other three died of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid">typhoid</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur,_French_biologist,_1878,_Paris_slnsw.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2122" data-file-width="1492" decoding="async" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Louis_Pasteur%2C_French_biologist%2C_1878%2C_Paris_slnsw.jpg/220px-Louis_Pasteur%2C_French_biologist%2C_1878%2C_Paris_slnsw.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Louis_Pasteur%2C_French_biologist%2C_1878%2C_Paris_slnsw.jpg/330px-Louis_Pasteur%2C_French_biologist%2C_1878%2C_Paris_slnsw.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Louis_Pasteur%2C_French_biologist%2C_1878%2C_Paris_slnsw.jpg/440px-Louis_Pasteur%2C_French_biologist%2C_1878%2C_Paris_slnsw.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur,_French_biologist,_1878,_Paris_slnsw.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Louis Pasteur, French biologist and chemist, 1878, by A Gerschel</div></div></div>
<p>Pasteur was appointed professor of chemistry at the University of Strasbourg in 1848, and became the chair of chemistry in 1852.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1854, to have time to carry out work that could earn him the title of correspondent of the Institute, he got three months' paid leave with the help of a medical certificate of convenience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> He extends the leave until 1 August, the date of the start of the exams. "I tell the Minister that I will go and do the examinations so as not to increase the embarrassment of the service. It is also so as not to leave to another a sum of 6 or 700 francs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In this same year 1854, he was named dean of the new faculty of sciences at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Lille" title="University of Lille">University of Lille</a>, where he began his studies on fermentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chirality_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chirality-41">[41]</a></sup> It was on this occasion that Pasteur uttered his oft-quoted remark: "<i>dans les champs de l'observation, le hasard ne favorise que les esprits préparés</i>" ("In the field of observation, chance favors only the prepared mind").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1857, he moved to Paris as the director of scientific studies at the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_Normale_Sup%C3%A9rieure" title="École Normale Supérieure">École Normale Supérieure</a></i> where he took control from 1858 to 1867 and introduced a series of reforms to improve the standard of scientific work. The examinations became more rigid, which led to better results, greater competition, and increased prestige. Many of his decrees, however, were rigid and authoritarian, leading to two serious student revolts. During "the bean revolt" he decreed that a mutton stew, which students had refused to eat, would be served and eaten every Monday. On another occasion he threatened to expel any student caught smoking, and 73 of the 80 students in the school resigned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debre_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Debre-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1863, he was appointed professor of geology, physics, and chemistry at the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%89cole_nationale_sup%C3%A9rieure_des_Beaux-Arts" title="École nationale supérieure des Beaux-Arts">École nationale supérieure des Beaux-Arts</a></i>, a position he held until his resignation in 1867. In 1867, he became the chair of organic chemistry at the Sorbonne,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debré_&amp;_Patrice_pp._505-7_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Debré_&amp;_Patrice_pp._505-7-44">[44]</a></sup> but he later gave up the position because of poor health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In 1867, the École Normale's laboratory of physiological chemistry was created at Pasteur's request,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debré_&amp;_Patrice_pp._505-7_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Debré_&amp;_Patrice_pp._505-7-44">[44]</a></sup> and he was the laboratory's director from 1867 to 1888.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In Paris, he established the Pasteur Institute in 1887, in which he was its director for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fein_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-fein-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-van-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_asymmetry">Molecular asymmetry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pcrystals.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="230" data-file-width="288" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Pcrystals.svg/220px-Pcrystals.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Pcrystals.svg/330px-Pcrystals.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Pcrystals.svg/440px-Pcrystals.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pcrystals.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pasteur separated the left and right <a href="/wiki/Crystal" title="Crystal">crystal</a> shapes from each other to form two piles of crystals: in solution one form rotated light to the left, the other to the right, while an <a href="/wiki/Racemic_mixture" title="Racemic mixture">equal mixture</a> of the two forms canceled each other's effect, and does not rotate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polarized_light" title="Polarized light">polarized light</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In Pasteur's early work as a <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a>, beginning at the <i>École Normale Supérieure</i>, and continuing at Strasbourg and Lille, he examined the chemical, optical and crystallographic properties of a group of compounds known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tartrates" title="Tartrates">tartrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flack_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flack-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>He resolved a problem concerning the nature of <a href="/wiki/Tartaric_acid" title="Tartaric acid">tartaric acid</a> in 1848.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gal_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gal-52">[52]</a></sup> A solution of this compound derived from living things <a href="/wiki/Optical_rotation" title="Optical rotation">rotated</a> the <a href="/wiki/Plane_of_polarization" title="Plane of polarization">plane of polarization</a> of light passing through it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flack_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flack-48">[48]</a></sup> The problem was that tartaric acid derived by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">chemical synthesis</a> had no such effect, even though its chemical reactions were identical and its elemental composition was the same.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohn_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-cohn-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pasteur noticed that crystals of tartrates had small faces. Then he observed that, in <a href="/wiki/Racemic_mixture" title="Racemic mixture">racemic mixtures</a> of tartrates, half of the crystals were right-handed and half were left-handed. In solution, the right-handed compound was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dextrorotatory" title="Dextrorotatory">dextrorotatory</a>, and the left-handed one was levorotatory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flack_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Flack-48">[48]</a></sup> Pasteur determined that optical activity related to the shape of the crystals, and that an asymmetric internal arrangement of the molecules of the compound was responsible for twisting the light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chirality_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chirality-41">[41]</a></sup> The (2<i>R</i>,3<i>R</i>)- and (2<i>S</i>,3<i>S</i>)- tartrates were isometric, non-superposable mirror images of each other. This was the first time anyone had demonstrated <a href="/wiki/Chirality_(chemistry)" title="Chirality (chemistry)">molecular chirality</a>, and also the first explanation of <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomerism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flack_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-Flack-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some historians consider Pasteur's work in this area to be his "most profound and most original contributions to science", and his "greatest scientific discovery."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flack_48-4"><a href="#cite_note-Flack-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermentation_and_germ_theory_of_diseases">Fermentation and germ theory of diseases</span></h3>
<p>Pasteur was motivated to investigate fermentation while working at Lille. In 1856 a local wine manufacturer, M. Bigot, whose son was one of Pasteur's students, sought for his advice on the problems of making beetroot alcohol and souring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup> Pasteur began his research in the topic by repeating and confirming works of <a href="/wiki/Theodor_Schwann" title="Theodor Schwann">Theodor Schwann</a>, who demonstrated a decade earlier that yeast were alive.
</p><p>According to his son-in-law, René Vallery-Radot, in August 1857 Pasteur sent a paper about lactic acid fermentation to the Société des Sciences de Lille, but the paper was read three months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> A memoire was subsequently published on 30 November 1857.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> In the memoir, he developed his ideas stating that: "I intend to establish that, just as there is an alcoholic ferment, the yeast of beer, which is found everywhere that sugar is decomposed into alcohol and carbonic acid, so also there is a particular ferment, a <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid_bacteria" title="Lactic acid bacteria">lactic yeast</a>, always present when <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid_fermentation" title="Lactic acid fermentation">sugar becomes lactic acid</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manchester2007_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-manchester2007-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pasteur also wrote about alcoholic fermentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> It was published in full form in 1858.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus von Liebig</a> had proposed the theory that fermentation was caused by decomposition. Pasteur demonstrated that this theory was incorrect, and that yeast was responsible for fermentation to produce alcohol from sugar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-61">[61]</a></sup> He also demonstrated that, when a different microorganism contaminated the wine, lactic acid was produced, making the wine sour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1861, Pasteur observed that less sugar fermented per part of yeast when the yeast was exposed to air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-61">[61]</a></sup> The lower rate of fermentation aerobically became known as the <a href="/wiki/Pasteur_effect" title="Pasteur effect">Pasteur effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1547" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg/220px-Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg/330px-Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg/440px-Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_experiment.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pasteur experimenting in his laboratory.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LouisPasteurMonumentLille.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2560" data-file-width="1760" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/LouisPasteurMonumentLille.jpg/220px-LouisPasteurMonumentLille.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/LouisPasteurMonumentLille.jpg/330px-LouisPasteurMonumentLille.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/LouisPasteurMonumentLille.jpg/440px-LouisPasteurMonumentLille.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LouisPasteurMonumentLille.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institut_Pasteur_de_Lille" title="Institut Pasteur de Lille">Institut Pasteur de Lille</a></div></div></div>
<p>Pasteur's research also showed that the growth of micro-organisms was responsible for spoiling beverages, such as beer, wine and milk. With this established, he invented a process in which liquids such as milk were heated to a temperature between 60 and 100 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowden_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden-63">[63]</a></sup> This killed most bacteria and moulds already present within them. Pasteur and <a href="/wiki/Claude_Bernard" title="Claude Bernard">Claude Bernard</a> completed tests on blood and urine on 20 April 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Pasteur patented the process, to fight the "diseases" of wine, in 1865.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowden_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden-63">[63]</a></sup> The method became known as <a href="/wiki/Pasteurization" title="Pasteurization">pasteurization</a>, and was soon applied to beer and milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nelson2009_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson2009-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beverage contamination led Pasteur to the idea that micro-organisms infecting animals and humans cause disease. He proposed preventing the entry of micro-organisms into the human body, leading <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister,_1st_Baron_Lister" title="Joseph Lister, 1st Baron Lister">Joseph Lister</a> to develop <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> methods in surgery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Listerine_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Listerine-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1866, Pasteur published <i>Etudes sur le Vin</i>, about the diseases of wine, and he published <i>Etudes sur la Bière</i> in 1876, concerning the diseases of beer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Agostino_Bassi" title="Agostino Bassi">Agostino Bassi</a> had shown that <a href="/wiki/Muscardine" title="Muscardine">muscardine</a> was caused by a fungus that infected silkworms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hatcher_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hatcher-67">[67]</a></sup> Since 1853, two diseases called <i><a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9brine" title="Pébrine">pébrine</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Flacherie" title="Flacherie">flacherie</a></i> had been infecting great numbers of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silkworm" title="Silkworm">silkworms</a> in southern France, and by 1865 they were causing huge losses to farmers. In 1865, Pasteur went to <a href="/wiki/Al%C3%A8s" title="Alès">Alès</a> and worked for five years until 1870.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berche_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berche-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Silkworms with pébrine were covered in corpuscles. In the first three years, Pasteur thought that the corpuscles were a symptom of the disease. In 1870, he concluded that the corpuscles were the cause of pébrine (it is now known that the cause is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microsporidian" title="Microsporidian">microsporidian</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hatcher_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hatcher-67">[67]</a></sup> Pasteur also showed that the disease was hereditary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keim_pp._87-88_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keim_pp._87-88-70">[70]</a></sup> Pasteur developed a system to prevent pébrine: after the female moths laid their eggs, the moths were turned into a pulp. The pulp was examined with a microscope, and if corpuscles were observed, the eggs were destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keim_pp._87-88_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keim_pp._87-88-70">[70]</a></sup> Pasteur concluded that bacteria caused flacherie. The primary cause is currently thought to be viruses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hatcher_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hatcher-67">[67]</a></sup> The spread of flacherie could be accidental or hereditary. Hygiene could be used to prevent accidental flacherie. Moths whose digestive cavities did not contain the microorganisms causing flacherie were used to lay eggs, preventing hereditary flacherie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spontaneous_generation">Spontaneous generation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coldecygne.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="439" data-file-width="655" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Coldecygne.svg/220px-Coldecygne.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Coldecygne.svg/330px-Coldecygne.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Coldecygne.svg/440px-Coldecygne.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coldecygne.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bottle en <i>col de cygne</i> (<a href="/wiki/Swan_neck_flask" title="Swan neck flask">swan-neck bottle</a>) used by Pasteur</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_Experiment.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Louis_Pasteur_Experiment.svg/220px-Louis_Pasteur_Experiment.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Louis_Pasteur_Experiment.svg/330px-Louis_Pasteur_Experiment.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Louis_Pasteur_Experiment.svg/440px-Louis_Pasteur_Experiment.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_Experiment.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Louis Pasteur’s pasteurization experiment illustrates the fact that the spoilage of liquid was caused by particles in the air rather than the air itself. These experiments were important pieces of evidence supporting the germ theory of disease.</div></div></div>
<p>Following his fermentation experiments, Pasteur demonstrated that the skin of grapes was the natural source of yeasts, and that sterilized grapes and grape juice never fermented. He drew grape juice from under the skin with sterilized needles, and also covered grapes with sterilized cloth. Both experiments could not produce wine in sterilized containers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>His findings and ideas were against the prevailing notion of <a href="/wiki/Spontaneous_generation" title="Spontaneous generation">spontaneous generation</a>. He received a particularly stern criticism from <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9lix_Archim%C3%A8de_Pouchet" title="Félix Archimède Pouchet">Félix Archimède Pouchet</a>, who was director of the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9um_d%27Histoire_Naturelle_de_Rouen" title="Muséum d'Histoire Naturelle de Rouen">Rouen Museum of Natural History</a>. To settle the debate between the eminent scientists, the French Academy of Sciences offered the Alhumbert Prize carrying 2,500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Francs" title="Francs">francs</a> to whoever could experimentally demonstrate for or against the doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magner_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magner-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pouchet stated that air everywhere could cause spontaneous generation of living organisms in liquids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In the late 1850s, he performed experiments and claimed that they were evidence of spontaneous generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-porter-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magner_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Magner-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Redi" title="Francesco Redi">Francesco Redi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lazzaro_Spallanzani" title="Lazzaro Spallanzani">Lazzaro Spallanzani</a> had provided some evidence against spontaneous generation in the 17th and 18th centuries, respectively. Spallanzani's experiments in 1765 suggested that air contaminated broths with bacteria. In the 1860s, Pasteur repeated Spallanzani's experiments, but Pouchet reported a different result using a different broth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berche_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Berche-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pasteur performed several experiments to disprove spontaneous generation. He placed boiled liquid in a flask and let hot air enter the flask. Then he closed the flask, and no organisms grew in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-porter-77">[77]</a></sup> In another experiment, when he opened flasks containing boiled liquid, dust entered the flasks, causing organisms to grow in some of them. The number of flasks in which organisms grew was lower at higher altitudes, showing that air at high altitudes contained less dust and fewer organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Pasteur also used <a href="/wiki/Swan_neck_flask" title="Swan neck flask">swan neck flasks</a> containing a fermentable liquid. Air was allowed to enter the flask via a long curving tube that made dust particles stick to it. Nothing grew in the broths unless the flasks were tilted, making the liquid touch the contaminated walls of the neck. This showed that the living organisms that grew in such broths came from outside, on dust, rather than spontaneously generating within the liquid or from the action of pure air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>
These were some of the most important experiments disproving the theory of spontaneous generation. Pasteur gave a series of five presentations of his findings before the French Academy of Sciences in 1881, which were published in 1882 as <i>Mémoire</i> <i>Sur les corpuscules organisés qui existent dans l'atmosphère: Examen de la doctrine des générations spontanées</i> (<i>Account of Organized Corpuscles Existing in the Atmosphere: Examining the Doctrine of Spontaneous Generation</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Pasteur won the Alhumbert Prize in 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-porter_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-porter-77">[77]</a></sup> He concluded that:</p><blockquote><p>Never will the doctrine of spontaneous generation recover from the mortal blow of this simple experiment. There is no known circumstance in which it can be confirmed that microscopic beings came into the world without germs, without parents similar to themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz-69">[69]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Silkworm_disease">Silkworm disease</span></h3>
<p>In 1865, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Baptiste_Dumas" title="Jean-Baptiste Dumas">Jean-Baptiste Dumas</a>, chemist, senator and former Minister of Agriculture and Commerce, asked Pasteur to study a new disease that was decimating <a href="/wiki/Bombyx_mori" title="Bombyx mori">silkworm</a> farms from the south of France and Europe, the <a href="/wiki/P%C3%A9brine" title="Pébrine">pébrine</a>, characterized on a macroscopic scale by black spots and on a microscopic scale by the "<a href="/wiki/Emilio_Cornalia" title="Emilio Cornalia">Cornalia</a> corpuscles". Pasteur accepted and made five long stays in <a href="/wiki/Al%C3%A8s" title="Alès">Alès</a>, between 7 June 1865 and 1869.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Initial_errors">Initial errors</span></h4>
<p>Arriving in Alès, Pasteur familiarized himself with pébrine and also<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> with another disease of the silkworm, known earlier<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> than pebrine: <a href="/wiki/Flacherie" title="Flacherie">flacherie</a> or dead-flat disease. Contrary, for example, to <a href="/wiki/Jean_Louis_Armand_de_Quatrefages_de_Br%C3%A9au" title="Jean Louis Armand de Quatrefages de Bréau">Quatrefages</a>, who coined the new word <i>pébrine</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Pasteur makes the mistake of believing that the two diseases were the same and even that most of the diseases of silkworms known up to that time were identical with each other and with pébrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> It was in letters of 30 April and 21 May 1867 to Dumas that he first made the distinction between pébrine and flacherie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>He made another mistake: he began by denying the "parasitic" (microbial) nature of pébrine, which several scholars (notably <a href="/wiki/Antoine_B%C3%A9champ" title="Antoine Béchamp">Antoine Béchamp</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> considered well established. Even a note published on 27 August 1866 by <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard-G%C3%A9rard_Balbiani" title="Édouard-Gérard Balbiani">Balbiani</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> which Pasteur at first seemed to welcome favourably<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> had no effect, at least immediately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> "Pasteur is mistaken. He would only change his mind in the course of 1867".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Victory_over_p.C3.A9brine"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Victory_over_pébrine">Victory over pébrine</span></h4>
<p>At a time where Pasteur had not yet understood the cause of the pébrine, he propagated an effective process to stop infections: a sample of chrysalises was chosen, they were crushed and the corpuscles were searched for in the crushed material; if the proportion of corpuscular pupae in the sample was very low, the chamber was considered good for reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> This method of sorting “seeds” (eggs) is close to a method that Osimo had proposed a few years earlier, but whose trials had not been conclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> By this process, Pasteur curbs pébrine and saves many of the silk industry in the Cévennes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Flacherie_resists">Flacherie resists</span></h4>
<p>In 1878, at the <i>Congrès international séricicole</i>, Pasteur admitted that "if pébrine is overcome, flacherie still exerts its ravages". He attributed the persistence of flacherie to the fact that the farmers had not followed his advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1884, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89douard-G%C3%A9rard_Balbiani" title="Édouard-Gérard Balbiani">Balbiani</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> who disregarded the theoretical value of Pasteur's work on silkworm diseases, acknowledged that his practical process had remedied the ravages of pébrine, but added that this result tended to be counterbalanced by the development of flacherie, which was less well known and more difficult to prevent.
</p><p>Despite Pasteur's success against pébrine, French sericulture had not been saved from damage. (See <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/S%C3%A9riciculture" title="fr:Sériciculture">fr:Sériciculture</a> in the French Wikipedia.)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunology_and_vaccination">Immunology and vaccination</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chicken_cholera">Chicken cholera</span></h4>
<p>Pasteur's first work on vaccine development was on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_cholera" title="Chicken cholera">chicken cholera</a>. He received the bacteria samples (later called <i>Pasteurella multocida</i> after him) from <a href="/wiki/Jean_Joseph_Henri_Toussaint" title="Jean Joseph Henri Toussaint">Henry Toussaint</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotkin_pp._35-36_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plotkin_pp._35-36-99">[99]</a></sup> He started the study in 1877, and by the next year, was able to maintain a stable culture using broths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-100">[100]</a></sup> After another year of continuous culturing, he found that the bacteria were less pathogenic. Some of his culture samples could no longer induce the disease in healthy <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chickens</a>. In 1879, Pasteur, planning for holiday, instructed his assistant, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Chamberland" title="Charles Chamberland">Charles Chamberland</a> to inoculate the chickens with fresh bacteria culture. Chamberland forgot and went on holiday himself. On his return, he injected the month-old cultures to healthy chickens. The chickens showed some symptoms of infection, but instead of the infections being fatal, as they usually were, the chickens recovered completely. Chamberland assumed an error had been made, and wanted to discard the apparently faulty culture, but Pasteur stopped him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Pasteur injected the freshly recovered chickens with fresh bacteria that normally would kill other chickens; the chickens no longer showed any sign of infection. It was clear to him that the weakened bacteria had caused the chickens to become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immune" title="Immune">immune</a> to the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Artenstein_p._75_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Artenstein_p._75-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>
In December 1880, Pasteur presented his results to the French Academy of Sciences as "<i>Sur les maladies virulentes et en particulier sur la maladie appelée vulgairement choléra des poules</i> (On virulent diseases, and in particular on the disease commonly called chicken cholera)" and published it in the academy's journal (<i><a href="/wiki/Comptes_rendus_de_l%27Acad%C3%A9mie_des_Sciences" title="Comptes rendus de l'Académie des Sciences">Comptes-Rendus hebdomadaires des séances de l'Académie des Sciences</a></i>). He attributed that the bacteria were weakened by contact with oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plotkin_pp._35-36_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plotkin_pp._35-36-99">[99]</a></sup> He explained that bacteria kept in sealed containers never lost their virulence, and only those exposed to air in culture media could be used as vaccine. Pasteur introduced the term "attenuation" for this weakening of virulence as he presented before the academy, saying:</p><blockquote><p>We can diminish the microbe’s virulence by changing the mode of culturing. This is the crucial point of my subject. I ask the Academy not to criticize, for the time being, the confidence of my proceedings that permit me to determine the microbe’s attenuation, in order to save the independence of my studies and to better assure their progress... [In conclusion] I would like to point out to the Academy two main consequences to the facts presented: the hope to culture all microbes and to find a vaccine for all infectious diseases that have repeatedly afflicted humanity, and are a major burden on agriculture and breeding of domestic animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-104">[104]</a></sup> </p></blockquote>
<p>In fact, Pasteur's vaccine against chicken cholera was not regular in its effects and was a failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthrax">Anthrax</span></h4>
<p>In the 1870s, he applied this immunization method to <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a>, which affected <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a>, and aroused interest in combating other diseases. Pasteur cultivated bacteria from the blood of animals infected with anthrax. When he inoculated animals with the bacteria, anthrax occurred, proving that the bacteria was the cause of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Many cattle were dying of anthrax in "cursed fields".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz-69">[69]</a></sup> Pasteur was told that sheep that died from anthrax were buried in the field. Pasteur thought that earthworms might have brought the bacteria to the surface. He found anthrax bacteria in earthworms' excrement, showing that he was correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz-69">[69]</a></sup> He told the farmers not to bury dead animals in the fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Pasteur had been trying to develop the anthrax vaccine since 1877, soon after Robert Koch's discovery of the bacterium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2532" data-file-width="2082" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg/220px-Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg/330px-Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg/440px-Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Albert_Edelfelt_-_Louis_Pasteur_-_1885.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Louis Pasteur in his laboratory</i>, painting by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Edelfelt" title="Albert Edelfelt">A. Edelfeldt</a> in 1885</div></div></div>
<p>On 12 July 1880, Henri Bouley read before the French Academy of Sciences a report from <a href="/wiki/Jean_Joseph_Henri_Toussaint" title="Jean Joseph Henri Toussaint">Henry Toussaint</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veterinary_surgeon" title="Veterinary surgeon">veterinary surgeon</a>, who was not member of the academy. Toussaint had developed anthrax vaccine by killing the bacilli by heating at 55 °C for 10  minutes. He tested on eight dogs and 11 sheep, half of which died after inoculation. It was not a great success. Upon hearing the news, Pasteur immediately wrote to the academy that he could not believe that dead vaccine would work and that Toussaint's claim "overturns all the ideas I had on viruses, vaccines, etc."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_104-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-104">[104]</a></sup> Following Pasteur's criticism, Toussaint switched to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbolic_acid" title="Carbolic acid">carbolic acid</a> to kill anthrax bacilli and tested the vaccine on sheep in August 1880. Pasteur thought that this type of killed vaccine should not work because he believed that attenuated bacteria used up nutrients that the bacteria needed to grow. He thought oxidizing bacteria made them less virulent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>
But Pasteur found that anthrax bacillus was not easily weakened by culturing in air as it formed spores – unlike chicken cholera bacillus. In early 1881, he discovered that growing anthrax bacilli at about 42 °C made them unable to produce spores,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bazin_p._196_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bazin_p._196-109">[109]</a></sup> and he described this method in a speech to the French Academy of Sciences on 28 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> On 21 March, he announced successful vaccination of sheep. To this news, veterinarian Hippolyte Rossignol proposed that the Société d'agriculture de Melun organize an experiment to test Pasteur's vaccine.  Pasteur signed agreement of the challenge on 28 April. A public experiment was conducted in May at Pouilly-le-Fort. 58 sheep, 2 goats and 10 cattle were used, half of which were given the vaccine on 5 and 17 May; while the other half was untreated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-111">[111]</a></sup> All the animals were injected with the fresh virulent culture of anthrax bacillus on 31 May. The official result was observed and analysed on 2 June in the presence of over 200 spectators. All cattle survived, vaccinated or not. Pasteur had bravely predicted: "I hypothesized that the six vaccinated cows would not become very ill, while the four unvaccinated cows would perish or at least become very ill."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-111">[111]</a></sup> However, all vaccinated sheep and goats survived, while unvaccinated ones had died or were dying before the viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> His report to the French Academy of Sciences on 13 June concludes:</p><blockquote><p>[By] looking at everything from the scientific point of view, the development of a vaccination against anthrax constitutes significant progress beyond the first vaccine developed by Jenner, since the latter had never been obtained experimentally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-:2-111">[111]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>Pasteur did not directly disclose how he prepared the vaccines used at Pouilly-le-Fort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bazin_p._196_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bazin_p._196-109">[109]</a></sup> Although his report indicated it as a "live vaccine",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-:2-111">[111]</a></sup> his laboratory notebooks show that he actually used <a href="/wiki/Potassium_dichromate" title="Potassium dichromate">potassium dichromate</a>-killed vaccine, as developed by Chamberland, quite similar to Toussaint's method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giese_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Giese-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohn_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-cohn-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>The notion of a weak form of a disease causing immunity to the virulent version was not new; this had been known for a long time for <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>. Inoculation with smallpox (<a href="/wiki/Variolation" title="Variolation">variolation</a>) was known to result in a much less severe disease, and greatly reduced mortality, in comparison with the naturally acquired disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Jenner" title="Edward Jenner">Edward Jenner</a> had also studied <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a> using <a href="/wiki/Cowpox" title="Cowpox">cowpox</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Vaccinia" title="Vaccinia">vaccinia</a></i>) to give cross-immunity to smallpox in the late 1790s, and by the early 1800s vaccination had spread to most of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>The difference between smallpox vaccination and <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_cholera" title="Chicken cholera">chicken cholera</a> vaccination was that the latter two disease organisms had been artificially weakened, so a naturally weak form of the disease organism did not need to be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Giese_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Giese-114">[114]</a></sup> This discovery revolutionized work in infectious diseases, and Pasteur gave these artificially weakened diseases the generic name of "<a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a>", in honour of Jenner's discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Koch%E2%80%93Pasteur_rivalry" title="Koch–Pasteur rivalry">Koch–Pasteur rivalry</a></div>
<p>In 1876, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Koch" title="Robert Koch">Robert Koch</a> had shown that <i><a href="/wiki/Bacillus_anthracis" title="Bacillus anthracis">Bacillus anthracis</a></i> caused anthrax.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Paolo_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-De_Paolo-119">[119]</a></sup> In his papers published between 1878 and 1880, Pasteur only mentioned Koch's work in a footnote. Koch met Pasteur at the Seventh <a href="/wiki/International_Medical_Congress" title="International Medical Congress">International Medical Congress</a> in 1881. A few months later, Koch wrote that Pasteur had used impure cultures and made errors. In 1882, Pasteur replied to Koch in a speech, to which Koch responded aggressively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ullmann_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-ullmann-15">[15]</a></sup> Koch stated that Pasteur tested his vaccine on unsuitable animals and that Pasteur's research was not properly scientific.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1882, Koch wrote "On the Anthrax Inoculation", in which he refuted several of Pasteur's conclusions about anthrax and criticized Pasteur for keeping his methods secret, jumping to conclusions, and being imprecise. In 1883, Pasteur wrote that he used cultures prepared in a similar way to his successful fermentation experiments and that Koch misinterpreted statistics and ignored Pasteur's work on silkworms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-De_Paolo_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-De_Paolo-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Swine_erysipelas">Swine erysipelas</span></h4>
<p>In 1882, Pasteur sent his assistant <a href="/wiki/Louis_Thuillier" title="Louis Thuillier">Louis Thuillier</a> to southern France because of an <a href="/wiki/Epizootic" title="Epizootic">epizootic</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swine_erysipelas" title="Swine erysipelas">swine erysipelas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Thuillier identified the bacillus that caused the disease in March 1883.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berche_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-Berche-68">[68]</a></sup> Pasteur and Thuillier increased the bacillus's virulence after passing it through pigeons. Then they passed the bacillus through rabbits, weakening it and obtaining a vaccine. Pasteur and Thuillier incorrectly described the bacterium as a figure-eight shape. Roux described the bacterium as stick-shaped in 1884.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rabies">Rabies</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_Vanity_Fair_8_January_1887.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="785" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Louis_Pasteur_Vanity_Fair_8_January_1887.jpg/170px-Louis_Pasteur_Vanity_Fair_8_January_1887.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Louis_Pasteur_Vanity_Fair_8_January_1887.jpg/255px-Louis_Pasteur_Vanity_Fair_8_January_1887.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Louis_Pasteur_Vanity_Fair_8_January_1887.jpg/340px-Louis_Pasteur_Vanity_Fair_8_January_1887.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Pasteur_Vanity_Fair_8_January_1887.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Captioned "<a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">Hydrophobia</a>", caricature of Pasteur in the London magazine <i><a href="/wiki/Vanity_Fair_(British_magazine)" title="Vanity Fair (British magazine)">Vanity Fair</a></i>, January 1887</div></div></div>
<p>Pasteur produced the first vaccine for <a href="/wiki/Rabies" title="Rabies">rabies</a> by growing the virus in rabbits, and then weakening it by drying the affected nerve tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartz_69-4"><a href="#cite_note-Schwartz-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-wood-122">[122]</a></sup> The rabies vaccine was initially created by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emile_Roux" title="Emile Roux">Emile Roux</a>, a French doctor and a colleague of Pasteur, who had produced a killed vaccine using this method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ligon_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Ligon-7">[7]</a></sup> The vaccine had been tested in 50 dogs before its first human trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> This vaccine was used on 9-year-old <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Meister" title="Joseph Meister">Joseph Meister</a>, on 6 July 1885, after the boy was badly mauled by a rabid dog.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohn_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-cohn-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-wood-122">[122]</a></sup> This was done at some personal risk for Pasteur, since he was not a licensed physician and could have faced prosecution for treating the boy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-van-47">[47]</a></sup> After consulting with physicians, he decided to go ahead with the treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wasik_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wasik-125">[125]</a></sup> Over 11 days, Meister received 13 inoculations, each inoculation using viruses that had been weakened for a shorter period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-126">[126]</a></sup> Three months later he examined Meister and found that he was in good health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wasik_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wasik-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trueman_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-trueman-127">[127]</a></sup> Pasteur was hailed as a hero and the legal matter was not pursued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-van-47">[47]</a></sup> Analysis of his laboratory notebooks shows that Pasteur had treated two people before his vaccination of Meister. One survived but may not actually have had rabies, and the other died of rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Pasteur began treatment of Jean-Baptiste Jupille on 20 October 1885, and the treatment was successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson_126-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-126">[126]</a></sup> Later in 1885, people, including four children from the United States, went to Pasteur's laboratory to be inoculated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wasik_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wasik-125">[125]</a></sup> In 1886, he treated 350 people, of which only one developed rabies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jackson_126-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-126">[126]</a></sup> The treatment's success laid the foundations for the manufacture of many other vaccines. The first of the Pasteur Institutes was also built on the basis of this achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohn_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-cohn-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_San_Michele" title="The Story of San Michele">The Story of San Michele</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Axel_Munthe" title="Axel Munthe">Axel Munthe</a> writes of some risks Pasteur undertook in the rabies vaccine research:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Pasteur himself was absolutely fearless. Anxious to secure a sample of saliva straight from the jaws of a rabid dog, I once saw him with the glass tube held between his lips draw a few drops of the deadly saliva from the mouth of a rabid bull-dog, held on the table by two assistants, their hands protected by leather gloves.</p></blockquote>
<p>Because of his study in germs, Pasteur encouraged doctors to sanitize their hands and equipment before surgery. Prior to this, few doctors or their assistants practiced these procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ignaz_Semmelweis" title="Ignaz Semmelweis">Ignaz Semmelweis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Lister" title="Joseph Lister">Joseph Lister</a> had earlier practiced hand sanitizing in medical contexts in the 1860s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span></h2>
<p>A French national hero at age 55, in 1878 Pasteur discreetly told his family never to reveal his laboratory notebooks to anyone. His family obeyed, and all his documents were held and inherited in secrecy. Finally, in 1964 Pasteur's grandson and last surviving male descendant, Pasteur Vallery-Radot, donated the papers to the <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Bibliothèque nationale de France">French national library</a>. Yet the papers were restricted for historical studies until the death of Vallery-Radot in 1971. The documents were given a catalogue number only in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1995, the centennial of the death of Louis Pasteur, a historian of science <a href="/wiki/Gerald_L._Geison" title="Gerald L. Geison">Gerald L. Geison</a> published an analysis of Pasteur's private notebooks in his <i>The Private Science of Louis Pasteur</i>, and declared that Pasteur had given several misleading accounts and played deceptions in his most important discoveries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geison1995_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geison1995-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Max_Perutz" title="Max Perutz">Max Perutz</a> published a defense of Pasteur in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Review_of_Books" title="The New York Review of Books">The New York Review of Books</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Based on further examinations of Pasteur's documents, French immunologist Patrice Debré concluded in his book<i> Louis Pasteur</i> (1998) that, in spite of his genius, Pasteur had some faults. A book review states that Debré "sometimes finds him unfair, combative, arrogant, unattractive in attitude, inflexible and even dogmatic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fermentation">Fermentation</span></h3>
<p>Scientists before Pasteur had studied fermentation. In the 1830s, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Cagniard-Latour" title="Charles Cagniard-Latour">Charles Cagniard-Latour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Traugott_K%C3%BCtzing" title="Friedrich Traugott Kützing">Friedrich Traugott Kützing</a> and Theodor Schwann used microscopes to study yeasts and concluded that yeasts were living organisms. In 1839, <a href="/wiki/Justus_von_Liebig" title="Justus von Liebig">Justus von Liebig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_W%C3%B6hler" title="Friedrich Wöhler">Friedrich Wöhler</a> and <a href="/wiki/J%C3%B6ns_Jacob_Berzelius" title="Jöns Jacob Berzelius">Jöns Jacob Berzelius</a> stated that yeast was not an organism and was produced when air acted on plant juice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1855, <a href="/wiki/Antoine_B%C3%A9champ" title="Antoine Béchamp">Antoine Béchamp</a>, Professor of Chemistry at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Montpellier" title="University of Montpellier">University of Montpellier</a>, conducted experiments with sucrose solutions and concluded that water was the factor for fermentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> He changed his conclusion in 1858, stating that fermentation was directly related to the growth of moulds, which required air for growth. He regarded himself as the first to show the role of microorganisms in fermentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manchester2007_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-manchester2007-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pasteur started his experiments in 1857 and published his findings in 1858 (April issue of <i>Comptes Rendus Chimie</i>, Béchamp's paper appeared in January issue). Béchamp noted that Pasteur did not bring any novel idea or experiments. On the other hand, Béchamp was probably aware of Pasteur's 1857 preliminary works. With both scientists claiming priority on the discovery, a dispute, extending to several areas, lasted throughout their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manchester-2001_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-manchester-2001-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, Béchamp was on the losing side, as the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BMJ" title="BMJ">BMJ</a></i> obituary remarked: His name was "associated with bygone controversies as to priority which it would be unprofitable to recall".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Béchamp proposed the incorrect theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microzyme" title="Microzyme">microzymes</a>. According to K. L. Manchester, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-vivisectionist" title="Anti-vivisectionist">anti-vivisectionists</a> and proponents of <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicine</a> promoted Béchamp and microzymes, unjustifiably claiming that Pasteur plagiarized Béchamp.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manchester2007_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-manchester2007-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Pasteur thought that <a href="/wiki/Succinic_acid" title="Succinic acid">succinic acid</a> inverted sucrose. In 1860, <a href="/wiki/Marcellin_Berthelot" title="Marcellin Berthelot">Marcellin Berthelot</a> isolated <a href="/wiki/Invertase" title="Invertase">invertase</a> and showed that succinic acid did not invert sucrose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnett_61-4"><a href="#cite_note-Barnett-61">[61]</a></sup> Pasteur believed that fermentation was only due to living cells. He and Berthelot engaged in a long argument subject of vitalism, in which Berthelot was vehemently opposed to any idea of vitalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hans_Ernst_August_Buchner" title="Hans Ernst August Buchner">Hans Buchner</a> discovered that <a href="/wiki/Zymase" title="Zymase">zymase</a> catalyzed fermentation, showing that fermentation was catalyzed by enzymes within cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Buchner" title="Eduard Buchner">Eduard Buchner</a> also discovered that fermentation could take place outside living cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthrax_vaccine">Anthrax vaccine</span></h3>
<p>Pasteur publicly claimed his success in developing the anthrax vaccine in 1881.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trueman_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-trueman-127">[127]</a></sup> However, his admirer-turned-rival <a href="/wiki/Jean_Joseph_Henri_Toussaint" title="Jean Joseph Henri Toussaint">Henry Toussaint</a> was the one who developed the first vaccine. Toussaint isolated the bacteria that caused chicken cholera (later named <i><a href="/wiki/Pasteurella" title="Pasteurella">Pasteurella</a></i> in honour of Pasteur) in 1879 and gave samples to Pasteur who used them for his own works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> On 12 July 1880, Toussaint presented his successful result to the French Academy of Sciences, using an attenuated vaccine against anthrax in dogs and sheep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Pasteur on grounds of jealousy contested the discovery by publicly displaying his vaccination method at Pouilly-le-Fort on 5 May 1881.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chevallier-Jussiau_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chevallier-Jussiau-149">[149]</a></sup> Pasteur then gave a misleading account of the preparation of the anthrax vaccine used in the experiment. He claimed that he made a "live vaccine", but used potassium dichromate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geison1995_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Geison1995-16">[16]</a></sup> to inactivate anthrax spores, a method similar to Toussaint's. The promotional experiment was a success and helped Pasteur sell his products, getting the benefits and glory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chevallier-Jussiau_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chevallier-Jussiau-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental_ethics">Experimental ethics</span></h3>
<p>Pasteur's experiments are often cited as against <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">medical ethics</a>, especially on his vaccination of Meister. He did not have any experience in medical practice, and more importantly, lacked a <a href="/wiki/Medical_license" title="Medical license">medical license</a>.
